Topped with krystal caviar,&nbsp; dressed up with a beetroot tree. On the side - even more&nbsp; caviar on a beetroot tuile. We are told there is no right or wrong way. Just dig in. Excellent textures here. The smokey cream goes so well with salty caviar. It’s powerful. I really like it. Our next wine is a 2012&nbsp; German riesling from Keller. It’s dry and fruity with nice minerality,&nbsp; making it a great choice for seafood. Enter our next course. Spider Crab. At the bottom we have a brown crab bisque,&nbsp;&nbsp; with a white crab salad finished with pink&nbsp; grapefruit foam presented in the shell. This is a famous dish in Spain’s basque country,&nbsp; close to where Chef Helene Darroze is from. And it’s absolutely fantastic. The sweetness of the crab, with a bit of&nbsp; acidity, and bitterness from the grapefruit. There was an exciting hint of spiciness as well. I really enjoyed this dish. The bitter grapefruit was so beautiful. Where I come from, we don’t do much bitter, so it was great to see it&nbsp; used so well in this dish. Masterfully balanced. We shot this episode just before Christmas 2023, so it’s been almost nine months since our visit. Yet, I still remember this&nbsp; dish as if it was yesterday. The service here at the Sommelier’s&nbsp; Table is relaxed but attentive. As Guiseppe says: “We don’t want to be the best,&nbsp; we want to be the favorite”. I love it. Next is a blind tasting. The shape of the bottle gives&nbsp; away part of the mystery. It looks like a riesling… and it is. A bit sweet but beautifully balanced. It turns out to be a 2020 Egon Muller&nbsp; Kabinett from the Mosel region. This release is special for The Connaught&nbsp; and even has its own custom label. It shows you the power they have&nbsp; here at this iconic property. Our next course is onion soup. It’s a French classic with a modern touch. On the bottom we have onion,&nbsp; cheese from sheep’s milk,&nbsp;&nbsp; and sourdough flower topped&nbsp; with fresh black truffle. I loved the deep umami here. Our next wine is a 1986 Spanish&nbsp; Rioja red from Marqués de Murrieta. Outstanding wine. Next to the table is a bread service&nbsp; with an interesting butter presentation. Followed by a new server. It’s the lady herself. Chef Helen Darroze. In her career she accomplished a lot. She has held the title of&nbsp; World’s Best Female Chef. Became an honorary knight&nbsp; in France’s Legion of Honor. And was the inspiration for the character&nbsp; Colette in the film Ratatouille. Believe it or not, she came&nbsp; close to NOT becoming a chef. She entered the fine dining world as an assistant&nbsp;&nbsp; to Super Chef Alain Ducasse at&nbsp; Le Louis the fifteenth in Monaco. And worked mostly in the back office. But Ducasse learned something about Helen. That she came from a legacy of chefs. Four generations to be exact. Soon, Ducasse had her convinced&nbsp; to join him in the kitchen. They worked side by side for 3 years before&nbsp; Helene returned to her family’s restaurant. Then in 1999, she opened&nbsp; Restaurant Helene Darroze in Paris. Earning two michelin stars by 2003. In 2008, the Connaught did an&nbsp; 80 million euro refurbishment. And named Helene Darroze as the new chef. Soon after opening, the restaurant was&nbsp; climbing the ranks of the Michelin guide, with one star in 2009, the second&nbsp; in 2011 and the third in 2021. These days, she splits her time between&nbsp; London and Paris overseeing three restaurants. Her one star in the south of France, the two star in Paris, and the three star here at the Connaught. Chef Helene is tableside to introduce&nbsp; one of her signature dishes. Scallop from Scotland. Prepared with tandoori spices&nbsp; balanced with a citrus confit,&nbsp;&nbsp; carrot, coriander and chicken broth. Bright acids, popping flavors, and&nbsp; spice that is good but not overpowering. Served with rutabaga puree, a beurre blanc sauce&nbsp; with seaweed and a little bergamot from Sicily. The fish could not be cooked any better. - Pinot Noir, Merlot, Cabernet Sauvignon, Shiraz, Syrah - Red Wine Guide: Kind: captions Language: en in the world of red wine grapes there's a little more competition for the top slot so a little less agreement about which one is at the top of the food chain but there are definitely three red grapes Pinot Noir Merlot and Cabernet Sauvignon that come from the burgundy and Bordeaux regions of France that are the absolute icons of classic style there is one grape that I would add to this major-league group though because of the importance that it's picked up just in the last 10 or 20 years and that's a grape called Syrah also from France but that we often know under its new identity in other parts of the world where they call it Shiraz so let's go to the beginning and look at the lightest one of these wines Pinot Noir is the grape variety that has been grown in Burgundy since the 12th century and this style is on the lighter end of the red wine spectrum even looking at it in the glass we can see it has a translucency to it that is because the grape has relatively thin skins and so it's delivery of color and flavor intensity to the wine is on the lighter end of the spectrum however the flavors that it does give are so seductive and desirable that Pinot Noir is that one of the most desired styles of wine on earth it is coveted and collectible it is a cool climate style that is a little brighter a little bit more in the red fruit aromatic spectrum things like cranberries and strawberries come to mind this is a lighter style of red that works well with some foods that you might normally think of pairing with a white wine seafoods and white meats for example when we come to the kissing cousins next that come from Bordeaux we have Merlot and Cabernet Sauvignon two grapes that are planted in the same region and that are distantly related when they're grown in cold places like France and Italy they often have a little bit of aromatic resemblance to them as well but once we plant them in sunnier warmer places like Chile for example here or California we get a little more divergence in their aromatics and it gets easier to tell them apart on the nose Merlot tends to give us a little bit more of an herbal quality to it a little bit less of the earthiness that we find in Pinot Noir and seems to be a little bit darker in its fruit range a little bit more like blackberries and plums and a little bit less like strawberries and cranberries when we encounter it from a cool play we often get a woodsy character to it almost like cedar or even roasted bell peppers and it is very well-suited to food and its distant relation Cabernet Sauvignon is definitely among the most famous and well-known grape varieties in the world it makes some of the finest wines of Bordeaux and is known for the long term aging potential that it can have this is due to the fact that Cabernet Sauvignon was a grape has smaller berries and thicker skins than Merlot and when we ferment it in the tank that gives us a double dose of all of the sources of color and flavor that we get in the wine aromatically Cabernet Sauvignon has some resemblance to Merlot in its fruit definitely in the dark fruit blackberry and cherry range but it adds to that herbal quality flavors that remind me of darker foods like liquorice and chocolate and coffee very much an intense powerhouse of a wine that will deliver a big oof of flavor if I was going to add another grape certainly my choice would be a grape called Syrah that also comes from another region of France called the Rhone however we most often encounter it these days under another name where it's called Shiraz Shiraz is the name given to the Syrah grape when it's grown in places like Australia and South Africa in the southern hemisphere however it is genetically identical to the grape we call Syrah in places like California and France Syrah has similar thick skins and density of color to what we find from Cabernet Sauvignon but usually does not quite have as much tannic structure and as a result is more often made in the drink menow style with a little bit more emphasis on forward fruit this style has a kind of blackberry pie and black pepper quality to it that makes it very appealing and friendly and delivers a heck of a lot of flavor per square inch when you serve this as a sauce on the side

From the very beginning, I have always been looking for value in wine. I love well-known, highly praised labels, too, and I enjoy them a lot. But the truth is, I simply cannot afford them regularly. Labels like Don Perin, Stella de Compalto, or Top Burgundy are now reserved for rare celebration moments. So today, I wanted to share some of my thoughts on value alternatives to world's great wines. No matter which variety is at the forefront, these wines tend to be fullbodied, lush and fruitdriven with a savory depth. And while I would absolutely say that Shatan Dup can age beautifully in the bottle, the reality is that most of these wines are enjoyed quite young. Why? simply because despite their powerful nature they are also very approachable in their youth driven by sweet ripe fruit and resolved tenon. So if you enjoy Shatanov Dup I encourage you to try wines just across the river those of lak. After all there must be a good reason why shatanu dup producers are investing in lak vineyards. Liak might not offer the same level of power and structure, although you definitely can find those examples there, too, but in general, these wines show more fruit and freshness in the glass with lively acidity and leaner, tighter body, and they usually come in at half the price, if not even less than your favorite shatin dup. Personally, I find reaching for Lak more and more these days. I guess not many wine enthusiasts need introduction into this style or wine- making region. Shabli for many, Shabli is one of the first fine wines they start their wine journey with. It's relatively affordable, usually unoaked, easy to understand, and easy to pronounce. For me too, it remains an unconditional classic, offering fresh acidity, bright although sometimes shy fruit while retaining depth and expression of terawa. And while I do believe that shabli is one of those classic burgundy appellations still offering relatively good value, the reality is finding a bottle under €20 is becoming increasingly difficult. In fact, some might even argue that their light body, restrained citrus and orchard fruit notes with added depth from Lee's aging make them an even better pairing for oysters than Shabli itself. When I started thinking about this video, Borderaux kept creeping into my mind as region producing some of the world's greatest and most expensive wines. But each time I quickly brushed the idea off because in my mind, Bordeaux as a single Albate vast wine making region can offer both ultra expensive collector wine as well as singledigit glugloo wines. So, in many ways, Berdo can actually be its own value alternative. Take a look at the recently confirmed 170 K bourgeoa estates. You will find plenty of wines from your favorite appilations below €20 mark. And importantly, these estates were not only evaluated for the consistency of quality, but also for their environmental responsibility. Now, I will not claim that these wines have the same aging potential and they for sure do not have the same impact on the secondary market. But if you love the classic Bordeaux style, structure, firm tenant backbone and evolving flavors that go from delicate fruit to earthy meaty complexity, then Bordeaux has so much to offer at incredibly reasonable prices. And it is not just the left bank. There are incredible value wines on the right bank too. Here I suggest looking beyond the well-known appellations of Santeon and Pomero which by the way can also offer plenty of value and explore their satellite appellations like Montine Centimeon and Land de Pomero. The potential of these teroirs is reflected in the fact that some of the most prestigious estates like Chateau Anjalus and Chateau Latur have invested in the vineyards here. Despite their amazing quality, their labels may not be the best value buys though, but there are plenty of other wines to choose from. The only downside is it is easy to lose focus. There are so many labels to choose from. But if you're willing to explore a bit, Bordeaux still holds some of the best hidden gems in the wine world. I have been puzzled by the Napa Cabernet Soven for the longest time. Not all of the price tags make sense to me. So, it felt only natural to look for value alternatives to Napa Cabernet. So, Napa usually offers that really glossy fruit with a lot of power and often quite a bit of wine making, but it kind of works. All that open, luscious, bursting fruit almost needs some of that oak to calm it or put it in a frame, if you will. Now, when thinking about alternatives, I had few options pop into my mind. Firstly, if we do not want to go too far geographically, Paso Robles offers an amazing quality for a fraction of the price. Just recently, I tasted through some of the Jour wines, and the value they offer is truly top. The price to quality ratio was almost hard to believe. But look also for South American classics like Chile's Mipo Valley for Cabernet Sovin or Argentina's Uko Valley for Malbec. These wines similarly offer rich and glossy fruit, concentration, and power, all at much friendlier price point. While some of the more affordable labels are made in a lighter, more fruit driven style meant for immediate consumption, there is no shortage of densely textured oakaged examples that offer similar aging potential to those of Napa cabs. And majority of these wines are impolitely affordable, especially when compared to Napa Cabernet Sovven. Now, Ria del Duero is one of the classic wine- making regions in Spain known for producing dense, fullbodied and highly structured red wines. I have come across many articles and blog posts suggesting that its great alternative and true it sometimes offers better value is Rioa. But while both regions rely heavily on tempano grape variety, I think stylistically they are quite far apart. It also shows just how diverse this grape variety can be and how much impact tua and wine- making style have. For me personally, if I were looking for alternatives within Spain, I would rather be looking towards Toro or Humilia wine-making regions. Toro is also tempano based and delivers a similar structure, density, and power as Ria delu, though perhaps without the same elegance often found in top examples of Ria's wines. Humila on the other hand is based on Monostel grape which also produces powerful rich and concentrated wines but with an added spicy layer to the ripe blue and black fruit profile. But for a true true value alternative, I would actually look just across the border to the Doro Valley in Portugal. It is the same river valley that continues west all the way to the Atlantic Ocean. And not all the grapes grown there are suited for port production. In fact, some of the vineyards will yield better quality steel red wines. And for those of you who particularly love tempano, look for the name tintorish, which is the local name for the grape here. Most wines will be blends though, offering rich ripe blue and red fruit, elevated yet ripe, silky tannins, and a floral depth with notes of lilac and bergamont. So here you go. the reason people keep sellers cool is because those reactions happen slower and in a more controlled situation in a warm environment like on your kitchen counter where it's 85 degrees in texas those changes are going to go a lot faster so if you'd like to age wine if you plan on aging wine it's important to have temperature control also you need to know that bottle size matters if you look at this six liter bottle it's going to age much slower than a magnum or a 750 or even a little 375. so if you have 375s at home drink them sooner than you would drink a bigger bottle i want to talk about what happens in aging and i've drawn a little graph that i want to show you and this graph on the x-axis has time and on the y-axis has concentration and the red line i drew is what i call fruit characteristics and in a white wine fruit characteristics maybe um like in a riesling or an esophageal blanc be melons and peaches and apricots aromas like that in a red wine it may be strawberries brambles those aromas start out very high when a wine is very young over time those aromas start to go away and as the wine gets 15 20 years old there won't be any fruit aromas left or very very subtle fruit aromas the blue line i drew i marked as aged characteristics those start out at zero when a wine is young and over time they'll start to increase and the important thing with this graph is that at any time you drink this wine it's going to have very different characteristics as a young wine it's going to be mainly fruit and very little age characteristics at four or five six years old it'll have some fruit and it'll have some age characteristics um you know in the 10-year period it might have a little bit of both but the fruit is starting to go away and at 15 20 30 years there's not going to be much fruit left and a lot of age and the reason that's important is because the biggest variable in aging is what do you like is the consumer's preferences people who have sellers and drink old wines all the time are used to the characteristics that you get with age and those characteristics in a white wine might be things like butterscotch almonds old apple core in a red wine they may be more like coffee olives tobacco cassis things like that and someone who likes old wines and likes age characteristics is going to like that and not like young wines they're not wine a vertical would be the same winery the same wine maybe the same vineyard designation three or four or five years in a row or three or four or five different years so if you're able to buy the same wine but in different vintages buy four or five different vintages have your friends over maybe put them in a bag put them in labeled glasses and do a tasting and compare them and see which ones you like see if you like old see how they've changed over time the other way is to buy a case of wine and every year open a bottle and see how those are changing and whether you like them and when you - 6 Basic Rules For Pairing Food With Wine (Video): Kind: captions Language: en Hi, I'm Rachel May and today we're going to talk about wine pairings. Now, there's certain elements of wine. There's sugar, acid, fruit, tannins, and alcohol. And there's also flavor components of food. There's fat, acid, salt, sweet, bitter, and texture. So, when we think about pairing, we need to think about how these flavor elements are going to work together. Let's talk about some of the basic rules of wine pairing. Rule number one, hors d'oeuvres need a dry rosé. The reason is a rosé combines the light crispness of a white with the fruitiness of a red. And if you have a table of hors d'oeuvres, there's many different flavors and textures that you need to accommodate. Rule number two, acid needs acid. So, any food that has a high acidic level, something you'd want to squeeze a lemon onto, that's a great pairing with a light citrusy acidic white wine. If you have a dish like chicken piccata, pasta with tomato sauce, or a roasted fish with citrus, which will pair with a bright citrusy Pinot Grigio or Sauvignon Blanc. It'll be like a zap of fresh lemon in your dish. Rule number three, tannins need fat. If you have food with a higher fat content, say a marbled ribeye steak, or a braised duck, or grilled sausages, you really need a wine with a textural element that will balance it out. If you pair with a bold red wine like a Syrah or a Cabernet, the tannins in that red wine really balance out the fat element. Rule number four, heat needs sweet. If you're serving a dish that has a little kick to it or a little bit of spice, you're going to need a lighter slightly sweet wine to pair with it. A good example would be a Riesling or a white Zinfandel. Rule number five, salty needs bubbles. Now, it can be a little hard to pair salty or fried foods with a wine, but similar to a beer, the carbonation of the sparkling wine adds a whole different texture and brings a whole new flavor to it. Rule number six, earthy needs earthy. Now, if you have a earthier food like mushrooms, lentils, or gamey meats, they're going to be great paired with an earthy wine. A good choice would be a Syrah or a Pinot Noir. You really want to think about your dominant flavors. Is it sweet? Is it salty? Is it citrusy? These are the elements you want to consider when pairing your wine with your entrée. Hopefully, this gives you a better idea for how to pair your wines with your food. These tips are brought to you by Shoptopia, just what you want, right where you are.

noir from close by the lament Valley Oregon rather than a white wine now if you're geographically challenged don't worry about it the following tips will definitely help you out the number 2 tip is body both red and white wines are on a continuum of being anything from very light to very full the same thing holds true for food you'll find everything from very light delicate foods to very full heavy dishes that have a lot going on when it comes to the body you want to match the weight of your food to the weight of your wine light food pairs best with light body wines heavy foods pair best with full-bodied wine i trigger the trade in determining this is by looking at the color of the food and the wine a meal with a light even Dockett sauce both in color and texture go well with light body wine if you have a heavy dark super Stu please go better with a full-bodied wine the number 3 tip is acid mirrors acid now acid is the element in wine that causes your mouth to water and prepares your palate for food food that have a high acidic level would pair well with light citrusy acidic white wines summer fruit salads chicken piccata spaghetti with a light not a full body but a light tomato sauce now rich dishes with butter or cream oily fish and buttery shellfish would work well with a fresh citrusy Pinot Grigio or a Sauvignon Blanc a white Bordeaux whites from Sancerre or maybe a Vouvray from Alsace France or Germany pairing with many of these wines would be like adding just the squeeze or just a touch of lemon to each of those dishes the number four tip is tannin loves fat tannin is the element in wine that dries your mouth out it kind of makes your your mouth pucker it tastes somewhat bitter food with a higher fat content like well marbled steak braised duck bratwurst or sausage will soften and balance out the tannin and the wine acidic foods like I mentioned a second ago clash with tannins you won't enjoy an acidic entree with a highly tuned antic wine the easiest way to remember repairing your meats and wines is the less marbled or less fat in the meat the light of the red wine the more fat the darker the red wine how are you doing there is this information making sense if it is right Asti in the comments below the number 5 tip is heat NEADS sweet those foods that have a fair amount or even a lot of kick to them your go-to wine would be a sweet or semi-sweet wine now I know I know some of you are saying I don't like sweet wines but I assure you with hot and spicy foods a Riesling or a Moscato is absolutely the way to go you'll be very pleasantly surprised now when it comes to sweet wines here's a couple more tips the wine needs to be sweeter than food there are all kinds of sweet dessert wines that will pair well with your after-dinner delights big red big tannin wines rarely successfully matched with sweets but if you're indulging in chocolate a Cabernet Sauvignon is absolutely the way to go the number six tip is salty and fried needs acidity and effervescent scent another way fried chicken love champagne no really I do this for a group down in the Florida Panhandle a while back they were absolutely crazy about it pairings bitter sweet salt fat and acid so let's say you have this dish with kale and kale has some bitterness in it you might want to compliment that bitterness with fat salt and some sweetness to help balance out the dish it's useful to note that the more intense the taste the more intense the balancing taste should be so if I had radicchio in the last example which is a lot more bitter I might opt for something like candied walnuts and blue cheese to really counterbalance the flavors the topic of flavor pairing goes pretty deep there are now other flavors we've learned and understood you're learning but as soon as you add wine it gets more complicated when I'm thinking about pairing wines with food I like to think of the wine more as an ingredient with attributes then as just wine itself wine as an ingredient is a fermented beverage with a lower pH which means fundamentally it's on the acid side of the spectrum next some wines are sweet and some are dry as in not sweet and finally if you're working with a red wine you will have an element of bitterness in the form of pigment and poly phenols aka tannin now that we see wine is an ingredient we will choose the role it plays with the food it will either be a congruent pairing where the flavors go together or a complementary pairing where they contrast one another a good example of a congruent pairing would be a zesty Sauvignon Blanc paired with chilled cucumber soup in this pairing the sharp citrusy and herbaceous flavors that Sauvignon Blanc highlight the freshness of the cucumber's in a soup which is texturally very creamy a good example of a complementary pairing would be the classic pairing of steak and Cabernet Sauvignon in this pairing the acids and tannins in the wine work opposite the rich fatty flavors and umami in the meat when you have a good understanding of the fundamental tastes in the different styles of wine it's actually pretty easy to come up with food and wine pairings on recommended pairings for these ingredients as you go across the x-axis dots indicate a pairing and large dots indicate an excellent pairing so if you want to create your own pairing identify the major ingredients in your dish the sort of the primary flavor profiles and then use the chart to find recommended wines so for example let's say I want a pet want to pair a BLT sandwich on wheat bread I It was clear something serious was going on. I had the time to do whatever I wanted, so I decided to leave at 3:00 a.m. and drive 600 km in my old Peugeot 204. It's about an 8-hour drive. I planned to drive through the night to get there at dawn and then back to work at Desau. As I drove along the motorway, I kept asking myself, "What am I doing? What's the point of this? What am I going to find?" And I kept driving and driving until finally I arrived in Pomer. I spent half an hour circling around before I finally saw Chat Fit painted on a wall. What a relief. The fog was starting to lift and I was exhausted. I pulled into the courtyard and then I saw a dog chained up and barking furiously and there was a man there looking quite rough with two missing front teeth. He asked, "What are you doing here? Who are you?" And I was confronted by this man who was obviously tasked with keeping the lady inside. Amidst the shouting, the shutters of the house began to open slowly and I saw a face just the nose and eyes and the head of an older woman with long straggly hair and a look of deep sadness and I said, "Hello, good morning." Immediately her eyes lit up. Here was a woman who had been held captive for months. Maybe here was a glimmer of hope, an English-sp speakaking person. Could it be possible? maybe he'll be able to help me. And I quickly asked her, "How are you, Madame Dog?" She said, "Oh, I'm so hungry and thirsty," speaking in both French and English at the same time. I went inside and saw everything scattered on the floor, dishes, clothes, shirts, photos. She said to me, "Since 1977, I've had everything taken from me. I was so starved. I had to remember the survival training from the American Commando courses that I'd taken." She told me I was collecting worms from the ditches in Pome, eating pine nuts that I gathered from umbrella pine trees, catching crickets and scavenging for whatever I could find here and there. I left for Paris thinking, "This is unbelievable. What did I just witness? What a story this old lady in Pomerall has." So I said to my friend, "Come back with me to Bordeaux and you can sit in the back with the old lady while I drive and we'll take turns. I returned to Bordeaux a week later, picked up Mrs. Dome, and drove her back to Paris. And since I just separated from my wife, there were no family complications to deal with. I had time to take care of this lady and be curious enough to find out what was going on and to host her at my place. I mean, she hadn't showered in 6 months. The smell was awful. Like a barnyard, we say. So, I took off her dirty, greasy Astroan coat and put it to one side. I put her in the bathtub and the water quickly turned into something that looked like manure because she was so filthy. She hadn't washed in 6 months. Just muck. My son came in and said, "Dad, what's going on with this old lady? Give me the scissors." I cut through her blouse, her clothes, even her underwear, and tossed everything into the laundry basket. And there she was, stuck naked. this poor old lady who was like, "Oh, in the bathtub making the most of it." I thought to myself, "This is quite a scene." I said to Jeremy, "Hand me those old pajamas and the old dressing gown." I gave her the dressing gown and we sat down to eat. I watched as this radiant old lady enjoyed her meal. We'd made a stew and I saw her eating and then hiding some bread. What was she doing? She was hiding pieces of bread, stashing them away whenever we weren't looking. She must have thought it might not last. I cared for this lady for six years from 79 to 85. Jeremy was 12 and had already started in secondary school. She taught him English. She was like his granny calling him my darling. We became a little family. Time went on in this way and I ended up becoming her legal representative. If anyone thought I was taking care of her out of self-interest, I can only say that when I first met her, I had no idea that Chateau Fit was hers through her late husband's inheritance. In 1985 she died from a blood clot at New York hospital and that's when my lawyer informed me Mr. Shasser she has named you the sole heir to the vineyards of chat fetit clean. I discovered who Jean Gabrielle Dome was, the famous painter known for his paintings of long necked women. I managed to recover a few of his less notable paintings from the attic as most of the valuable ones had been looted. I had Madame Dome laid to rest in Azha in the family tomb carved and sculpted by the painter himself, complete with a magnificent crest depicting the family patriarch. It was then lying in pieces. As time passed, my son grew up and became an anologist. To honor her memory, I decided we needed to do something special. So instead of calling it Chat Fet, we created a Ku Marg. One year, we had labels printed in her honor and crafted a special cu named Ku Maris Dome. Over the 40 years I've been collecting wine, I've crossed paths with hundreds of people. Just to give you an idea, I've got all the business cards from those encounters right here. And there are still just as many here. I end up sorting them into little piles by country, by champagne. Sometimes I get a call. Hello, this is Jepierre. Jean Pierre? Sure, but which Jeepierre? I know seven of them. He's got no idea. I don't have a secretary or a computer. Everything's organized in the oldfashioned way. If I had all the resources to fulfill my dreams, it wouldn't be to buy a Rolls-Royce or a private jet. Instead, I'd build a kind of louv for wine. A grand international museum for posterity, a place to teach, to showcase to the public, a cultural center for wine tasting. So, here are the plans for my wine museum. I'm just waiting on the building permit. You see, that's my house. The current seller holds 40,000 bottles, but the plan is to extend it into a 50 m long space that would reach all the way to the little path over there. And in 2000, I renovated the cellar you see now. By 2020, it was full again. So, I'm hoping this one will last until 2041 when I'll turn 100. When I think about it, I've got 6,000 of these slats with 25,000 screws. Oh, the work. 500 partition walls to place side by side, 30 cm apart to store the cases and build 4,000 compartments. It's true that lately I've been gaining more and more global recognition, but there's a downside. One morning, bright and early, I was kidnapped, held hostage, and tortured by 11 thugs. I was sleeping peacefully in bed around 7 or 8:00 when the doorbell rang. Dingdong, ding, dong. A delivery this early? What's going on? It's usually later. So, I slipped into my pajamas and rushed to the door, thinking I might miss the delivery if I'm not quick. I opened the door, saw no one around, and as I stepped outside, I spotted a truck parked against the wall with someone inside. I said, "What's going on?" They replied, "It's a wine delivery." The guy sat down a wine box and said, "Now sign the delivery slip." And just as I bent down to pick up the box, the UPS truck, part of the whole setup, opened up and out popped four guys with AK-47s shouting, "Don't move or you're dead." And at that moment, I thought, "So, today's the day." Because I'd been hearing for ages, "With all the talk about your wine celler, you're bound to get robbed eventually." They dragged me into the first cellar. I saw only four of them initially, but there were 11 thugs in total. They'd come to steal some cases of wine with four trucks from up north for their boss. They took me down into the first small cellar and started hitting me, demanding, "Where's the key to the armored cellar?" They tortured and beat me up. At one point, they said, "Hold on. And then I had this huge brute, a guy about six feet tall, right in front of my face, twisting my fingers until I was screaming. The others could hear me shouting from a distance, let me tell you. In the end, my little finger was so messed up, the nerves were torn, and it was flailing around uncontrollably. Another one came with two screwdrivers and put one each side of my neck and he said, "I'm going to stab you in the neck if you don't give me the key." Damn it. You're desperately trying to find a way to survive. And it seemed like I was growing more resilient to the torture. Since my hands were already bound together with duct tape, I kept repeating, "Oh God, help me. Oh God, help me. Oh God, help me." They couldn't kill me, otherwise they'd leave empty-handed, so I wasn't afraid of dying. But in my mind, it was all about preventing them from getting through the armored door. And then they realized they weren't going to break me. So they left, locking me inside the cellar with the heavy oak door and its massive bolt. So there I was, trapped in my own cellar behind that godforsaken door. I thought to myself, maybe this is my chance to escape. But I knew that as soon as I tried to get away, they'd spot me and say, "Look, he's trying to escape." And I'd end up getting beaten even more. So I had to think quickly. The situation was about survival. Maybe I could break down the door. I had a heavyduty strut filled with glass that I sometimes use when I'm away for a week. I grabbed it and struck the door. Bang. But that oak door would not budge. I tried hitting the lower hinge. Bang. Bang. I managed to squeeze through a hole like a rat. And when I got out, there was nobody around. I ran up the stairs. And then they said, "Well, we need to search your house. I said, "Wait, it's 7:00 p.m. I have friends coming to visit my little wine seller. They'll be upset if I'm not there." And by 3:00 a.m., I fell asleep as if nothing had happened. Yes, most wines are meant to be drunk, but perhaps these particular bottles are more valuable than that. as they represent memories, history, and geography. They have many stories to tell. We shouldn't drink them. What happens once the bottle is empty? The story is gone. If a wine from the Napoleonic era leaves its bottle, then Napoleon himself disappears along with it. Hello, brother. This book is a catalog of all the plants used in making the chart leur from Tarragona in Spain. It was considered a medicinal elixir back then. To treat the monks isolated in their Spanish monastery for 175 years, they relied on centuries of trial and error, mixing various plants to cure all sorts of ailments. They ended up using over 130 different plants. But on top of that, there are roots, lychans, and even insect wings. Nowadays, chhatros is made in Varon, France, but it's just a copy made with various crops. Most of the original plants used in the chhatros deagona no longer exist due to pollution. - Why Does My Wine Cellar Make Odd Noises? - Fine Wine Facts: Kind: captions Language: en Why does my wine seller make odd noises? Imagine walking into your wine celler and hearing strange sounds coming from the cooling system. It might make you wonder if something is wrong with your wine storage. Many wine sellers, especially those with built-in cooling units, produce noises that are perfectly normal. These sounds are part of how the system keeps your wine at the right temperature and humidity. Most cooling systems in wine sellers include a compressor, fans, and refrigerant lines. The compressor is like the heart of the system, and it often makes a humming or buzzing noise when it turns on. This sound is similar to what you hear from a refrigerator, but can be louder if your seller is very quiet. The fans are responsible for circulating air and maintaining even temperatures. As they run, they can produce a whooshing or worring sound. Sometimes you might hear bubbling or gurgling noises. These are caused by the refrigerant moving through the pipes, especially when the system is cooling down. This is a normal part of the cooling process and does not indicate a problem. You might also notice popping or creaking sounds. These happen when the materials in the cooling unit expand or contract because of temperature changes. This is common in wine sellers where the temperature fluctuates slightly. If the cooling unit is not mounted securely, it can vibrate and create rattling or vibrating sounds. Ensuring the unit is firmly attached and the surface is stable can help reduce this noise. If you hear loud humming or buzzing from the fans, it could be because they are dirty or blocked by dust or labels. Cleaning the fans and removing any obstructions can quiet things down. Clanking or metallic noises might mean the compressor has shifted or is not sitting properly on its mounts. This may requiring a technician to check and fix. Keeping your wine seller in good shape is important for your wine collection. Odd noises can sometimes mean the cooling system is working harder than it should. If the sounds suddenly become very loud or are accompanied by poor cooling or ice buildup, it's wise to check for blocked vents, dirty coils, or refrigerant leaks. Regular maintenance, like cleaning the fans and coils, making sure the unit is level, and leaving enough space around it for air flow, can help keep your cellar quiet. When your cooling system runs smoothly, it helps maintain the ideal conditions for aging wine. That way, your wine stays in perfect shape and your cellar remains a peaceful place to enjoy your collection.

- How Much Humidity Does A Luxury Wine Cellar Need? - Luxury Life Report: Kind: captions Language: en [music] How much humidity does a luxury wine seller need? Imagine having a collection of fine wines stored perfectly, aging gracefully in a space designed just for them. The secret to keeping those bottles in top shape is controlling the environment, especially the humidity. So, how much humidity should a luxury wine seller have? Well, the ideal range is between 50% and 70% relative humidity. Most experts agree that setting it at around 60% is best for long-term storage. Why is this important? Because wine corks need the right moisture level to stay supple and seal the bottle properly. If humidity drops below 50%, corks can dry out, letting air seep in and causing oxidation. That air can spoil the wine and ruin its flavor. On the other hand, if humidity rises above 70%, mold can develop. Mold can damage labels, the seller walls, and even the bottles. For collectors who want their wines to look as good as they taste, maintaining this balance is key. To do this, many luxury wine sellers use advanced climate control systems. These units carefully regulate both temperature and humidity, preventing sudden changes that could harm the wine. In dry climates, a built-in humidifier adds moisture to keep the environment stable. In humid areas, cooling units remove excess moisture through condensation or drainage, stopping mold from forming. Proper insulation and vapor barriers are also part of a high-end seller's construction. These features help keep the humidity steady and block outside air from messing with the environment. The racks and shelves are made from moisture resistant materials, which help protect the collection and keep the cellar looking beautiful. For those who want to keep a close eye on conditions, highquality sensors are a must. These devices give real-time readings, so adjustments can be made quickly if needed. Lighting is another detail to consider. Lead lights are preferred because they produce little heat and won't affect the humidity or temperature. So, in short, a luxury wine seller should stay between 50 and 70% humidity with 60% being the sweet spot. Achieving this involves smart climate control, solid construction, and regular monitoring. Doing so keeps your collection safe, preserves the wine's quality, and ensures your investment ages just right. >> [music]

- How to Host the Perfect Wine Tasting Party | Beginners Guide: Kind: captions Language: en-US Ever wanted to host a wine tasting party but didn't know where to start? Stay tuned, because today I'm going to show you how to host the perfect wine tasting party that will impress your friends and make you the ultimate wine connoisseur. Hello, wine lovers! Welcome back to Toast to Wine. From grape to glass, we're here to demystify and instruct on wine varieties, winemaking techniques, and the art of enjoying wines. Today, we're diving into the exciting world of wine tasting parties. Whether you're a seasoned wine enthusiast or a complete beginner, this guide will help you host a memorable and enjoyable wine tasting event. Let's start with the basics...selecting the wines. And by the way, this video is packed with great tips and ideas all the way to the end. So get your note pad ready! Continuing on... For a beginner's wine tasting party, I recommend choosing a variety that showcases different styles and regions. Aim for a mix of reds, whites, and maybe even a sparkling wine to keep things interesting. You don't need to break the bank; there are plenty of affordable options that are perfect for tasting. For example, if you're looking for a crisp, refreshing white, try a Sauvignon Blanc from New Zealand, like Oyster Bay, which usually costs around twelve to fifteen dollars. If you prefer something a bit richer, a California Chardonnay like Bogle Vineyards is a great choice, typically priced around ten to thirteen dollars. On the red side, a light Pinot Noir from Oregon, such as Mark West, offers a lot of flavor for around ten to twelve dollars. If you want something bolder, consider a Malbec from Argentina, like Alamos, which can be found for nine to eleven dollars. Don't forget about sparkling wines! A Prosecco from Italy, like La Marca, is an excellent choice that's often available for thirteen to sixteen dollars. These selections are not only budget-friendly but also provide a diverse tasting experience for your guests. Once you've selected your wines, it's time to open them. If your bottle has a traditional cork, remember to twist the corkscrew gently and pull the cork out with a slow, steady hand. Pour just enough wine to fill about a third of the glass - this allows the wine to breathe and gives your guests enough room to swirl it around, releasing those wonderful aromas. But what if your wine has a screw cap instead of a cork? There's often a stigma around screw top caps, with some people thinking they're a sign of lower-quality wine. In reality, screw caps are perfectly fine and, in many cases, better at preserving the wine's freshness. They're easy to open and don't require any special tools, making them a convenient option for your tasting party. So don't hesitate to include wines with screw caps - they're just as enjoyable and can still offer a fantastic tasting experience. Now, let's talk about the order of tasting. Typically, you'll want to start with lighter wines and progress to heavier ones. This means beginning with sparkling or white wines and then moving on to reds. When it comes to reds, start with lighter varieties, like a Pinot Noir. Pinot Noir, such as one from Oregon's Willamette Valley, is a great choice for its delicate flavors and smooth finish. As you move through the tasting, you can gradually introduce bolder reds. A good next step might be a Merlot, which has a bit more body but is still approachable, like a bottle from Columbia Crest in Washington State. Finally, finish with the boldest reds, like a Cabernet Sauvignon or a Sir ah. A California Cabernet, such as one from Napa Valley's Robert Mondavi, offers rich flavors and complexity that can stand up to a wide range of foods. This progression helps to keep your palate fresh and allows you to fully appreciate the nuances of each wine. No wine tasting is complete without the right pairings. Cheese, fruits, nuts, and even chocolate can enhance the flavors of the wines you're serving. Arrange these snacks on a platter so your guests can experiment with different combinations. For white wines, a crisp Sauvignon Blanc pairs beautifully with tangy goat cheese or fresh green apple slices. If you're serving a Chardonnay, try pairing it with creamy Brie or roasted nuts - the buttery notes of the wine complement these rich flavors perfectly. For reds, start with lighter options like Pinot Noir. This wine pairs wonderfully with mild, earthy cheeses like Gruyère or Camembert. You could also serve it with dried cherries or strawberries, which highlight the wine's fruity undertones. As you move to bolder reds, like a Merlot or Cabernet Sauvignon, consider pairing them with stronger, aged cheeses like sharp Cheddar or blue cheese. Dark chocolate is another excellent match for Cabernet, enhancing its deep, rich flavors. For something heartier, pair these wines with savory bites like charcuterie, especially salami or prosciutto - the possibilities are truly endless. To make your wine tasting even more interactive, provide tasting cards for your guests. These cards can include the name of the wine, its origin, and space for notes on aroma and taste. Encourage your guests to jot down their impressions - it's a fun way to see how everyone's palate differs! it's time to taste! Start by observing the color of the wine - hold your glass up to the light and take note of its clarity and hue. Is the color pale and golden, or deep and ruby-red? This can hint at the wine's age and variety. Next, gently swirl the wine in your glass. As the wine settles, observe the 'legs' or 'tears' that form on the inside of the glass. These are the streaks of liquid that run down after swirling, and they can give you an idea of the wine's alcohol content and viscosity - thicker, slower-moving legs usually indicate a higher alcohol content or sugar level. bring the glass to your nose and take a deep sniff. With white wines, you might notice fresh, citrusy notes like lemon or green apple, floral scents like honeysuckle, or even tropical fruits like pineapple. Reds, on the other hand, often present aromas of dark fruits like blackberries or cherries, spices like cinnamon or pepper, and sometimes earthy notes like leather or tobacco. take a sip and let the wine linger on your palate before swallowing. Try to identify the flavors - are they crisp and zesty, like you'd expect from a white wine, or rich and bold, as is common with reds? Also, pay attention to the wine's texture - is it light and refreshing, or full-bodied and smooth? Consider the finish as well - how long do the flavors last, and what notes linger as the wine fades? Hosting a wine tasting party is all about enjoying the experience, learning something new, and most importantly, having fun with friends. Don't stress too much about the details - after all, wine is meant to be enjoyed! So gather your friends, pour some wine, and savor the journey of flavors. Thanks for joining me on this beginner's guide to hosting a wine tasting party. I hope you're feeling inspired and ready to host your own event. Questions? Leave them in the comments section below. If you found this guide helpful, please give it a thumbs up and share it with your fellow wine lovers. Click on one of these videos now to continue your wine journey. If you found this video helpful, please give it a thumbs up, subscribe to our channel, and hit the bell icon to stay updated with our latest videos. If you have any questions or topics you'd like us to cover, leave a comment below. Until next time, may your wine adventures be as rich and diverse as the wines you discover. Toast to Wine!

But in reality, a smaller vineyard selling moderately priced wines consistently over time is often far more profitable. Those ultra-rare, one-bottle sales might make headlines, but the vineyards offering quality bottles at $10 to $50 a pop are playing the long game and bringing in way more steady revenue. It’s the difference between a one-off luxury splash and sustainable, inclusive success.
